<div class="markdown"><h1>Global evaluation of PlantBiophysics.jl : Taylor diagram</h1>
<p>This Pluto notebook presents the computation of Fig. 4 from the scientific article. The notebook computes a Taylor diagram using results computed and stored in non-Pluto script available <a href="https://github.com/VEZY/PlantBiophysics-paper/blob/main/tutorials/Fig4_PlantBiophysics_TaylorDiag_noPluto.jl">here</a>.</p>
<h2>Importing the dependencies:</h2>
</div>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>begin
    using Plots, CSV, DataFrames, Colors, Measures,TaylorDiag, PlotlyJS
    using PlantBiophysics
    plotlyjs()
end</code></pre>
<pre id='var-Colors' class='code-output documenter-example-output'>Plots.PlotlyJSBackend()</pre>

<div class="markdown"><h2>Computing statistics</h2>
</div>


<div class="markdown"><p>In order to display a Taylor diagram, we need two statistics: </p>
<ul>
<li><p>standard deviation &#40;i.e. &quot;amplitude&quot; of evaluated data&#41;;</p>
</li>
<li><p>correlation &#40;i.e. pattern similarity between two datasets&#41;.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Standard deviations are normalized in order to represent all variables &#40;<span class="tex">$A$</span>, <span class="tex">$E$</span>, <span class="tex">$T_l$</span>, <span class="tex">$G_s$</span>&#41; on the same diagram.</p>
</div>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>begin
    VARS=["A","E","Tl","Gs"]
    Ss=zeros((4,3))
    Cs=zeros((4,3))

    for i in eachindex(VARS)
        var=VARS[i]

        # Loading observations and simulations for all three packages
        obs   = filter(x-&gt;(cmp.(x.variable,var)==0)&(cmp.(x.origin,"PlantBiophysics.jl")==0),df_res).measured
        modPB = filter(x-&gt;(cmp.(x.variable,var)==0)&(cmp.(x.origin,"PlantBiophysics.jl")==0),df_res).simulated
        modPE = filter(x-&gt;(cmp.(x.variable,var)==0)&(cmp.(x.origin,"plantecophys")==0),df_res).simulated
        modLG = filter(x-&gt;(cmp.(x.variable,var)==0)&(cmp.(x.origin,"LeafGasExchange.jl")==0),df_res).simulated

        # Computing standard deviations
        S = [STD(modPB), STD(modPE), STD(modLG)]
        S = [S[i]/STD(obs) for i in eachindex(S)] # Normalization
        Ss[i,:]=S

        # Computing correlations
        C = [COR(obs,modPB),COR(obs,modPE),COR(obs,modLG)]
        Cs[i,:]=C
    end
end</code></pre>



<div class="markdown"><h2>Plotting</h2>
</div>


<div class="markdown"><h3>Plotting parameters</h3>
</div>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>begin
    # Colors for each package
    col_pb = (244, 124, 124)    # PlantBiophysics.jl
    col_lg = (93, 174, 139)     # LeafGasExchange.jl
    col_pe = (112, 161, 215)    # plantecophys
    transparency_col = 0.6      # Transparency for edges
    transparency_fill = 0.4     # Transparency for fill
    color_pb = rgb(col_pb..., transparency_col)
    color_pe = rgb(col_lg..., transparency_col)
    color_lg = rgb(col_pe..., transparency_col)
    fill_pb = rgb(col_pb..., transparency_fill)
    fill_pe = rgb(col_lg..., transparency_fill)
    fill_lg = rgb(col_pe..., transparency_fill)
    
    cols = [fill_pb,fill_lg,fill_pe] 
    strkcols = [color_pb,color_lg,color_pe] 
    
    # Parameters for fitting
    msize = 7                   # Marker size
    stw = 1.5                   
    legend_lab_size=10
    xleg = 1.6                  # x-position for variables legend
    yleg = 0.4                  # y-position for variables legend
    nms = ["PlantBiophysics.jl","plantecophys","LeafGasExchange.jl"]
end
</code></pre>
<pre id='var-fill_pe' class='code-output documenter-example-output'>3-element Vector{String}:
 "PlantBiophysics.jl"
 "plantecophys"
 "LeafGasExchange.jl"</pre>


<div class="markdown"><h3>Plotting Taylor diagram</h3>
<p>Using the same Taylor diagram for all the data is correct thanks to STD normalization.</p>
</div>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>begin
    # Plotting initial Taylor diagram
    fig = taylordiagram([1],[1],[""],normalize=true,ang=pi/2,rmsd_circ=false,figsize=600)

    # Adding data label
    Plots.scatter!([1],[0],label="Data",markerstrokecolor=:black,markercolor=:black)
    plot!(size = (600,600))
    mks=[:circle, :rect, :diamond, :utriangle]
    

    for n in eachindex(mks)
        rho   = Ss[n,:]
        theta = to_polar(Cs[n,:])
            for i in 1:length(theta)
                if n==1
                    lab=nms[i]
                else
                    lab=""
                end
                Plots.scatter!([cos.(theta[i]).*rho[i]],[sin.(theta[i]).*rho[i]],markerstrokecolor=strkcols[i],markercolor=cols[i],markershape=mks[n],markersize=msize,markerstrokewidth=stw,label=lab)
            end
        plot!(fontfamily="NotoSans-Regular.ttf",fontsize=12)
        scatter!([xleg],[yleg-0.1*(n-1)], markerstrokecolor=:grey,markercolor=:grey,markershape=mks[n],markersize=msize,markerstrokewidth=stw, markerstrokealpha=0.6,markeralpha=0.4,label="")
        annotate!(xleg+0.05, yleg-0.1*(n-1)-0.005, text(string(VARS[n]), :left, legend_lab_size))
    end
    plot!(legend=:topright, foreground_color_legend = nothing)
end</code></pre>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>Plots.savefig(fig, "./out/figure_global_simulation_taylor.png")</code></pre>
<pre id='var-hash138277' class='code-output documenter-example-output'>"/home/runner/work/PlantBiophysics-paper/PlantBiophysics-paper/tutorials/out/figure_global_simulation_taylor.png"</pre>


<div class="markdown"><h3>Explanation</h3>
<p>Taylor diagrams are tools invented and published in the 90s-00s allowing better visualisation of model realism evaluation. Via a clever use of relationship between RMSE, standard deviation and correlation, the Taylor diagram illustrates the accuracy of a model in polar coordinates. </p>
<p>Here, the <span class="tex">$\rho$</span> axis represents normalized standard deviation. 1 is the normalized standard deviation of observed data. If a modeled data point is higher &#40;lower&#41; than 1, it means that model induces higher &#40;lower&#41; standard deviation than observed data. For example, green diamond here has a more realistic standard deviation than blue diamond, i.e. blue diamond data tends to have exaggerated amplitudes with respect to data.</p>
<p>The <span class="tex">$\theta$</span> axis represents correlation, i.e. the pattern similarity between observed and modeled data. Two datasets may have similar standard deviation but very different correlations &#40;for instance if a dataset is highly dephased&#41;.</p>
<h4>Toy example</h4>
</div>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>begin
    Lx=100
    x=range(0,10,Lx)
    obs=sin.(x)
    mod_lowSTD=1.2*sin.(x)+sin.(x).*rand(Lx)*0.2
    mod_verylowSTD=1.6*sin.(x)+sin.(x).*rand(Lx)*0.2
    mod_lowCOR=sin.(x.+0.3).+sin.(x).*rand(Lx)*0.2
    mod_verylowCOR=sin.(x.+0.6).+sin.(x).*rand(Lx)*0.2

    Plots.plot(x,obs,label="Obs.",linewidth=3)
    plot!(x,mod_lowSTD,label="Mod. with low STD resolution")
    plot!(x,mod_verylowSTD,label="Mod. with very low STD resolution")
    plot!(x,mod_lowCOR,label="Mod. with low correlation resolution")
    plot!(x,mod_verylowCOR,label="Mod. with very low correlation resolution")
    plot!(legend=:outertopright)
    plot!(size = (650,400))
end</code></pre>

<div class="markdown"><p>Here, we represent 5 datasets:</p>
<ul>
<li><p><code>Obs</code> is the target dataset, a sinusoide.</p>
</li>
<li><p><code>Mod. with low STD resolution</code> the same than &#39;Obs&#39; but with noise and amplified amplitude.</p>
</li>
<li><p><code>Mod. with very low STD resolution</code> the same than &#39;Obs&#39; but with noise and even more amplified amplitude.</p>
</li>
<li><p><code>Mod. with low correlation resolution</code> the same than &#39;Obs&#39; but with noise and shifted.</p>
</li>
<li><p><code>Mod. with very low correlation resolution</code> the same than &#39;Obs&#39; but with noise and even more shifted.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Each &quot;modeled&quot; dataset is designed to illustrate behaviours on the Taylor diagram presented below.</p>
</div>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>begin
    nmes=["Obs","LowSTD","VeryLowSTD","LowCOR","VeryLowCOR"]
    S=[STD(obs),STD(mod_lowSTD),STD(mod_verylowSTD),STD(mod_lowCOR),STD(mod_verylowCOR)]
    S=S/STD(obs)
    C=[COR(obs,obs),COR(obs,mod_lowSTD),COR(obs,mod_verylowSTD),COR(obs,mod_lowCOR),COR(obs,mod_verylowCOR)]
    p = taylordiagram(S,C,nmes,rmsd_circ=false,normalize=true)
end</code></pre>

<div class="markdown"><p>As expected, the Taylor diagram highlights the main failures of the datasets. The low and very low STD resolution ones have good correlation coefficients &#40;near the <span class="tex">$y&#61;0$</span> axis&#41; but are far away from the <code>Obs</code> point for standard deviation &#40;distant from the center&#41;. The low and very low COR resolution ones have similar distance from center than the <code>Obs</code> point but move away from the <span class="tex">$y&#61;0$</span> axis &#40;lower correlation coefficients&#41;.</p>
</div>


<div class="markdown"><h2>References</h2>
</div>

<pre class='language-julia'><code class='language-julia'>"""
    rgb(r, g, b, a)

Like `Colors.RGBA` but accepts colors in the 0-255 range.
"""
function rgb(r, g, b, a)
    return RGBA(r / 255, g / 255, b / 255, a)
end</code></pre>
